intermediate
8 min
Abu Hanifah's Proof on Tanned Skin Compensation: The Core Dispute
Presents Abu Hanifah's rational proof and rebuttal to Sahibayn on liability for a tanned skin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Abu Hanifah's argument that the value from tanning is subsidiary to the craftsmanship, and explain why the Sahibayn disagreed on the owner's right to claim the full tanned value.

Core Idea
Value from the usurper's craftsmanship is not guaranteed, so the skin's added value follows: owner cannot claim full value.

In this passage from the Book of Usurpation, Imam Abu Hanifah presents a rational proof (dalil aqli) addressing a specific dispute within the Hanafi school. The question: when a usurper tans a valueless dead skin, does the owner have the right to abandon the now-valuable tanned skin and claim its full value from the usurper? Abu Hanifah argues no, because the value arises from the usurper's craftsmanship, which is not guaranteed. The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, disagree. This lesson walks through the proof and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What is the specific legal question? It is whether the owner of a usurped skin that has been tanned by the usurper can abandon the skin and claim its full value from the usurper.
The skin initially had no value (dead skin).
2
State Imam Abu Hanifah's argument
Abu Hanifah argues that the value (taqawwum) was created by the usurper's craftsmanship. The craftsmanship is valuable but not guaranteed (ghayr madmun). Since the skin's value is subsidiary to the craftsmanship, it is also not guaranteed. Therefore, the owner cannot claim the value of the tanned skin.
Key: the skin is a subsidiary (tabi') in terms of value.
3
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn) and their proof
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the owner may abandon the skin and claim its full value. They argue that the tanned skin must be returned (wajib al-radd) because it is the owner's property, and the tanning adds value, making the skin guaranteed. They analogize to a dyed cloth (thawb masbug) where the owner can claim the full value.
Their proof relies on the obligation to return property.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifah's rebuttal to Sahibayn
Abu Hanifah responds: the obligation to return follows ownership, which existed before tanning, so the skin is not subsidiary to craftsmanship in terms of ownership. The analogy with dyed cloth fails because that cloth had value before dyeing, unlike the dead skin.
Two different contexts: guarantee (daman) vs. return (radd).
5
Conclude the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifah, the owner cannot claim full value; the usurper may detain the skin until he recovers his expenses. According to Sahibayn, the owner may claim full value. The majority (al-aktharun) follow Abu Hanifah.
This difference affects how compensation is calculated in practice.

Book of Usurpation. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifah: He said: Taqawwum (being valuable property) occurred by the act of the usurper, and his craftsmanship is valuable because he used valuable property in it; for this reason he has the right to detain it until he recovers the increase from tanning, so it is his right, and the skin is subsidiary to him in respect of being valuable. Then the original (the craftsmanship) is not guaranteed upon him, so likewise the subsidiary, as when it perishes without his act. This is contrary to the obligation of return when it is extant, because that follows ownership, and the skin is not subsidiary to the craftsmanship in respect of ownership because ownership was established before it, even if it was not valuable. This is contrary to the slaughtered skin and the cloth, because their being valuable was established before tanning and dyeing, so they were not subsidiary to the craftsmanship. If it is extant and the owner wishes to leave it with the usurper and claim its value, it is said that he does not have that right according to Abu Hanifah, and according to them (the two companions) he has that right, because when he leaves it with him and claims the value, the usurper becomes unable to return it, so it becomes like destruction. And this is based on the difference we have explained. Then it is said: He guarantees its value as a tanned slaughtered skin, and if he tanned it with something valueless like soil or sun, it belongs to the owner for nothing, because it is like washing a garment. If the usurper destroys it, he guarantees its value as tanned, and it is said as pure (slaughtered) untanned, because the attribute of tanning is what he produced, so he does not guarantee it. The first view is held by the majority, because the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin, so it is not separated from it, and when the original becomes guaranteed upon him, so too its attribute. [Urdu translation follows similarly, with commentary.]
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the liability for a tanned skin that was usurped. The passage is a proof (daliil) from Imam Abu Hanifah and a rebuttal to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The core issue: When a usurper (ghasib) tans a dead skin (which was initially valueless), does the owner have the right to claim the tanned skin's full value from him? Abu Hanifah argues that the value (taqawwum) is created by the usurper's craftsmanship, so the skin is subsidiary to that craftsmanship in terms of value. Since the craftsmanship is not guaranteed (i.e., the usurper is not liable for it if the skin perishes without his act), the subsidiary (the skin) is also not guaranteed for the added value. Therefore, the owner cannot claim the full value of the tanned skin and simply abandon it to the usurper; rather, he must return the skin, and the usurper has the right to detain it until he recovers his expenses for the tanning. The two companions disagree: they argue that the tanned skin must be returned (وَلَهُ أَنَّهُ وَاجِبُ الرَّدِّ), and if the owner wishes to leave it with the usurper and claim its value, he may do so, because the tanning enhances the skin's value, making it guaranteed. The text then discusses various scenarios: if the skin is extant, the owner's option to abandon it and claim value; if the usurper destroys it, the measure of compensation; if the tanning material is valueless (like soil or sun), the tanned skin belongs to the owner without payment. The majority of later scholars (مشائخ) favor Abu Hanifah's position, holding that the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin and cannot be separated, so when the original skin is guaranteed, its attribute also follows. The commentary (تشریح) explains each point in Urdu, emphasizing the distinction between guarantee (ضمان) depending on being valuable (تقوم) versus return depending on ownership (ملک).

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): Being valuable property, i.e., having a recognized market value according to Islamic law.
صنعة (san'ah): The act of craftsmanship or processing, such as tanning or dyeing, which adds value to an object.
مضمون (madmun): Guaranteed, i.e., subject to liability for compensation if lost or damaged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifah, what is the reason the owner of a dead skin that was tanned by a usurper cannot abandon the tanned skin to the usurper and claim its full value?
A) Because the dead skin was not valuable before tanning, so the value belongs entirely to the usurper.
B) Because the skin is the owner's property, and the usurper's tanning does not change ownership.
C) Because the value (taqawwum) is created by the usurper's craftsmanship, and the craftsmanship is not guaranteed, so its subsidiary (the skin's value) is also not guaranteed.
D) Because the usurper has the right to keep the skin as compensation for his labor.

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifah argues that value created by the usurper's craftsmanship is not guaranteed, so the owner cannot claim the enhanced value of the tanned skin.

Applying the Ruling: Extant Skin, Destruction, and Valueless Tanning
Provides specific applications of the intra-Hanafi dispute on tanned skin compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifah's principle to determine compensation when a tanned skin is extant, destroyed, or tanned with valueless materials.

Core Idea
The subsidiary rule determines compensation: extant skin→owner cannot claim full value; destroyed→value of tanned skin; valueless tanning→skin belongs to owner.

After establishing the core principle, the text moves to specific applications: when the tanned skin is still extant, when the usurper destroys it, and when the tanning material is valueless. These scenarios test the understanding of Abu Hanifah's subsidiary rule and the majority position. This lesson walks through each scenario and its legal consequence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the core principle
Abu Hanifah's argument: the value from tanning is subsidiary to the craftsmanship, which is not guaranteed. Therefore, the owner cannot claim the full value of the tanned skin while the skin exists.
This is the foundation for all scenarios.
2
Apply to the extant skin scenario
If the tanned skin is still extant and the owner wishes to abandon it to the usurper and claim its value: According to Abu Hanifah, he cannot. The usurper has the right to detain the skin until he recovers his tanning expenses. According to Sahibayn, he may do so.
The dispute remains; the majority of later scholars follow Abu Hanifah.
3
Apply to the destruction scenario
If the usurper destroys the tanned skin, he guarantees its value as a tanned skin (or as an untanned slaughtered skin according to some). The reasoning: destruction makes the skin guaranteed, and the added value from tanning is included because the skin is destroyed as a whole.
Some say the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin, so when the skin is guaranteed, its attribute is also guaranteed.
4
Apply to the valueless tanning scenario
If the tanning material is valueless (e.g., soil or sun), the tanned skin belongs to the owner for nothing, because the act is like washing a garment. The usurper has no claim.
The craftsmanship is negligible, so no increase in value is recognized.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The majority (الأكثرون) follow Abu Hanifah's view that the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin. Therefore, when the original skin becomes guaranteed (e.g., by destruction), its attribute (the tanning value) is also guaranteed.
This resolution settles the practical application.

Book of Usurpation. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifah: He said: Taqawwum (being valuable property) occurred by the act of the usurper, and his craftsmanship is valuable because he used valuable property in it; for this reason he has the right to detain it until he recovers the increase from tanning, so it is his right, and the skin is subsidiary to him in respect of being valuable. Then the original (the craftsmanship) is not guaranteed upon him, so likewise the subsidiary, as when it perishes without his act. This is contrary to the obligation of return when it is extant, because that follows ownership, and the skin is not subsidiary to the craftsmanship in respect of ownership because ownership was established before it, even if it was not valuable. This is contrary to the slaughtered skin and the cloth, because their being valuable was established before tanning and dyeing, so they were not subsidiary to the craftsmanship. If it is extant and the owner wishes to leave it with the usurper and claim its value, it is said that he does not have that right according to Abu Hanifah, and according to them (the two companions) he has that right, because when he leaves it with him and claims the value, the usurper becomes unable to return it, so it becomes like destruction. And this is based on the difference we have explained. Then it is said: He guarantees its value as a tanned slaughtered skin, and if he tanned it with something valueless like soil or sun, it belongs to the owner for nothing, because it is like washing a garment. If the usurper destroys it, he guarantees its value as tanned, and it is said as pure (slaughtered) untanned, because the attribute of tanning is what he produced, so he does not guarantee it. The first view is held by the majority, because the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin, so it is not separated from it, and when the original becomes guaranteed upon him, so too its attribute. [Urdu translation follows similarly, with commentary.]
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the liability for a tanned skin that was usurped. The passage is a proof (daliil) from Imam Abu Hanifah and a rebuttal to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The core issue: When a usurper (ghasib) tans a dead skin (which was initially valueless), does the owner have the right to claim the tanned skin's full value from him? Abu Hanifah argues that the value (taqawwum) is created by the usurper's craftsmanship, so the skin is subsidiary to that craftsmanship in terms of value. Since the craftsmanship is not guaranteed (i.e., the usurper is not liable for it if the skin perishes without his act), the subsidiary (the skin) is also not guaranteed for the added value. Therefore, the owner cannot claim the full value of the tanned skin and simply abandon it to the usurper; rather, he must return the skin, and the usurper has the right to detain it until he recovers his expenses for the tanning. The two companions disagree: they argue that the tanned skin must be returned (وَلَهُ أَنَّهُ وَاجِبُ الرَّدِّ), and if the owner wishes to leave it with the usurper and claim its value, he may do so, because the tanning enhances the skin's value, making it guaranteed. The text then discusses various scenarios: if the skin is extant, the owner's option to abandon it and claim value; if the usurper destroys it, the measure of compensation; if the tanning material is valueless (like soil or sun), the tanned skin belongs to the owner without payment. The majority of later scholars (مشائخ) favor Abu Hanifah's position, holding that the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin and cannot be separated, so when the original skin is guaranteed, its attribute also follows. The commentary (تشریح) explains each point in Urdu, emphasizing the distinction between guarantee (ضمان) depending on being valuable (تقوم) versus return depending on ownership (ملک).

Key Terms
تقوم (taqawwum): Being valuable property, i.e., having a recognized market value according to Islamic law.
صنعة (san'ah): The act of craftsmanship or processing, such as tanning or dyeing, which adds value to an object.
مضمون (madmun): Guaranteed, i.e., subject to liability for compensation if lost or damaged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurper tans a dead skin using only sunlight (no cost), and then destroys it, what does the majority hold regarding his liability?
A) No liability, because the tanning was valueless.
B) He guarantees the value of the untanned skin (zero).
C) He guarantees the value as a tanned skin.
D) He must pay the owner the cost of the skin's replacement.

Key Takeaway
In application, the majority (الأكثرون) hold that the attribute of tanning is subsidiary to the skin, so the owner cannot claim full value when the skin is extant, and upon destruction the usurper guarantees the tanned value.

Tanning a Usurped Hide: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn on Retention Rights
Commentary expanding the Hidayah's text on a disputed fiqh case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the tanning of a usurped hide and apply the principle of tābi‘ (subordination) to similar cases of overlapping ownership and value.

Core Idea
Liability follows value, return follows ownership – the hide yields to craftsmanship in compensation but not in property.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The case involves a dead hide that is usurped and then tanned with a valuable substance. Who gets the tanned hide? The answer hinges on the intricate relationship between ownership, value, and legal subordination (tābi‘). This lesson will dissect the arguments and clarify the operative principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and the legal question
The scenario: A person usurps a dead animal hide (which has no commercial value), then tans it using a valuable substance. The legal question: Can the usurper retain the tanned hide until he is compensated for the increase in value?
Focus on the distinction between the hide’s pre-tanning value (zero) and post-tanning value (positive).
2
State Abu Hanifa’s position and his evidence
Abu Hanifa holds that the usurper has the right to retain the hide until the owner pays for the added value. His proof: liability (ḍamān) is based on value (taqawwum); the value here came from the usurper’s craftsmanship, which is not liable (ghayr mamnūn). Since the value is subordinate (tābi‘) to the craftsmanship, the hide’s value follows the craftsman’s immunity from liability.
Key: The hide itself is liable to return (ownership basis), but the value increment is not liable because it originates from the usurper’s lawful (or at least non-tortious) action.
3
State the Sahibayn’s position and their evidence
The Sahibayn argue that the hide must be returned to its owner unconditionally, and the usurper only gets compensation for the tanning. Their proof: return is obligatory (wājib al-radd) because ownership is prior; the owner’s right in the hide is not extinguished by tanning. They analogize to a dyed garment: if a garment is usurped and dyed, the owner gets the garment back and pays for the dyeing.
The analogy hinges on the fact that both the hide and the garment have owners, but the crucial difference is that the garment had value before the act, while the hide did not.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and the refutation of the analogy
The commentary refutes the Sahibayn’s analogy by pointing out that a garment already had commercial value before dyeing, so the value increase is not independent of the owner. In contrast, the dead hide had no value before tanning; its entire market value comes from the usurper’s craftsmanship. Thus, the hide’s value is wholly subordinate to the craftsman’s act, and liability for the value increment does not attach to the usurper.
This analysis uses the principle of tābi‘: the subordinate follows the principal. For liability, the principal is the source of value; for return, the principal is ownership.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: the owner cannot reclaim the tanned hide without paying the usurper the cost of tanning; the usurper may hold the hide as a lien. Under the Sahibayn: the owner may reclaim the hide immediately, but must pay the usurper for the tanning. The latter is the more practical approach and is often preferred by later Hanafi authorities.
The dispute is not about ownership but about temporary retention rights.

تشریح قوله وله ان التقوم .... الخ - امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں جو تقوم حاصل ہوا ہے وہ غاصب کی صنعت کی وجہ سے ہوا ہے بایں معنی کہ اس نے کھال میں ایک قیمتی مال استعمال کیا ہے اس لئے غاصب کو یہ اختیار ہے۔ اگر کھال اس وقت تک روکے رکھے کہ اس کی دباغت میں جو کچھ زیادتی ہوئی ہے وہ حاصل کرلے پس یہ اس کا حق ہے تو کھال تقوم کے حق میں تابع ہوئی اور اصل یعنی صنعت غاصب غیر مضمون ہے تو تابع بھی غیر مضمون ہوگا جیسے اگر وہ فعل غاصب کے بغیر خود تلف ہو جائے تو بالاتفاق ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔
قوله بخلاف وجوب الردة.... الخ - صاحبین کے قول و لانه واجب الرد “ کا جواب ہے کہ بخلاف واپسی واجب ہونے کے کہ یہ تو ملک کے تابع ہے اور کھال ملکیت کے حق میں دباغت کے تابع نہیں کیونکہ ملکیت تو غاصب کی صنعت سے پہلے ثابت تھی اگر چ قیمتی نہیں تھی حاصل یہ کہ ضمان کا اعتماد تقوم پر ہے اور تقوم میں اصل صنعت ہے اور صنعت مضمون نہیں تو جو اس کے تابع ہے وہ بھی مضمون نہ ہوگا اور واپسی کا اعتماد ملک پر ہے اور ملک میں اصل کھال ہے تو اس کی واپسی واجب ہوگی اور صنعت اس کے تابع ہوگی اور صاحبین کے قول كما اذا غصب ثوبا کا جواب یہ ہے کہ مذبوح کھال اور کپڑے کا قیمتی ہوناد باغت دینے اور رنگنے سے پہلے ثابت تھا تو وہ صنعت غاصب کے تابع نہ ہوا۔
The mashayikh say: Its reason is that the attribute of tanning is subordinate to the hide, so it will not be separated from it. And when the original hide is made liable (mamnoon) upon it, then likewise its attribute will also be. Explanation of his statement 'and he has the option due to the acquisition of value...': Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the value that has been acquired here is due to the usurper's craftsmanship, meaning he used a valuable substance on the hide, therefore the usurper has this option. If he keeps the hide until he recovers the increase that has occurred through tanning, then this is his right. So the hide became subordinate in terms of value, and the original (craftsmanship) of the usurper is not liable, so the subordinate is also not liable. For example, if it perishes on its own without the usurper's action, by consensus he is not liable. His statement 'contrary to the obligation of return...': The response to the Sahibayn's statement 'because it is obligatory to return' is that unlike the obligation of return, which is subordinate to ownership, and the hide in terms of ownership is not subordinate to tanning because ownership was established before the usurper's craftsmanship, even if it was not valuable. The conclusion is that liability is based on value, and in value the original is craftsmanship, and craftsmanship is not liable, so whatever is subordinate to it will also not be liable. And return is based on ownership, and in ownership the original is the hide, so its return is obligatory, and craftsmanship is subordinate to it. And the response to the Sahibayn's analogy (as when one usurps a garment) is that the carcass hide and the garment had value before tanning and dyeing, so they did not become subordinate to the usurper's craftsmanship.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) regarding the ruling on a hide usurped and then tanned with a valuable substance. The commentary expands the Hidayah's matn. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that since the value of the tanned hide comes from the usurper's action (tanning with a valuable substance), the usurper has the right to retain the hide until he recovers the increase in value. The key principle is that liability (daman) is based on value (taqawwum), and value here originates from the craftsmanship, which is not liable (ghayr mamnoon). Therefore, the hide's value is subordinate to the craftsmanship, and if the hide perishes unintentionally, the usurper is not liable. The Sahibayn counter that the hide must be returned to the owner because ownership was already established before tanning, and the obligation to return is based on ownership, not value. The commentator refutes this by distinguishing between liability (which follows value) and return (which follows ownership). The tanned hide is not analogous to a dyed garment because the hide had no value before tanning, whereas the garment had value before dyeing.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful appropriation of another's property without right.
دباغت (dibāgha): Tanning; the process of treating a hide to make it usable.
تابع (tābi‘): Subordinate; something that follows the ruling of the principal.
تقوم (taqawwum): Acquisition of value; becoming legally valuable.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation or guarantee.
واجب الرد (wājib al-radd): Obligatory to return the usurped property to its owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa in the passage, what is the basis for the usurper's right to retain the tanned hide?
A) The hide has become his property through tanning
B) The value added by his craftsmanship gives him a right until compensated
C) The owner has abandoned the hide
D) The Sahibayn agree with this view

Key Takeaway
In ghasb, liability and return are based on different principles: return follows ownership, but liability for value follows the source of value, which can lead to a retention right when the entire value is created by the tortfeasor.
